A breakout in a small town, attempted containment, people lose their minds, small group of survivors...been there, done that. I think it's safe to say my expectations weren't really that high. 'Just lay it on me', I thought as the movie began.What I got, surprisingly or not, exceeded my expectations. Although I don't think this remake surpasses the brilliance of the intelligent, small budget horror of 28 Days Later, it certainly rises to the same level. The tension is as omnipresent as it is relentless - there's never a dull moment (and every time you think there is, malice seems just around the corner). The zombies in this film work their magic by not actually appearing en-masse on screen - what Eisner has beautifully done is to take advantage of the simple fear of the unknown. You fear what you cannot see, and that's also how it is in The Crazies. Many scenes try to conceal the present evil in one way or another, and you don't see masses of zombies as you would in aforementioned DOTD remake. After the breakout, you mostly see them only half, or for brief moments (save one or two scenes). Add a sprinkle of black humor, and it seems to do the trick.Granted, it may not be as fun to watch with a group of drunken friends as the rest of the zombie arsenal out there, but it may perfectly well serve as a proper 'starter' for when everyone is still sober. Go and see this.</fullReview>

		<username>hyperballad13</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Predictable Yarn</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When people think of the Horror genre, people don't automatically think masterpieces or award winners. In fact, a lot of people tend to disapprove in the violent films and dismay every time someone is ripped into bloody pieces on screen. This being said, as a fan of Horror, one expects as much originality as you do with classic films. In recent years, however, once one film produces a stand out concept the trend is to repeat, remake and remodel until we are truly bored. The Crazies , a remake of a 1979 film of the same name, is unfortunately a film that has caught this plague. In The Crazies, a virus contaminates the water supply of small town Ogden Marsh turning nearly all the residents, well, crazy. As the folk of Ogden Marsh slowly turn into blood hungry killing machines, the Sheriff David Dutton and his gun wheedling deputy Russell Clank must fight to survive not just the "Crazies" but the Army who will contain the virus by any means possible. Dutton has no choice but to try to save his life, save his wife, and save his town. The premise of The Crazies had a lot of potential. Instead of turning people into brain eating zombies, this virus turns them into psychopathic murderers that then eventually die. However, this tiny scrap of originality does not make up for the overall lack of surprise. The format is practically the same as any other virus movie and within 5 minutes of watching the film, it is not hard to know who is going die, survive and what is going to happen next. Unfortunately, being able to correctly predict the storyline (and maybe some of the script too) makes it hard to feel excited. Alas, by the end you are wallowing in tedium and not suspense. The characters are underdeveloped too and instead of focusing on making them 3-Dimensional, the writers opt to kick start to drama far too early in the film. This means an hour and a half of watching characters you couldn't care less about trying to fight for their lives. To make this worse, as though to distract us from the poor plot, the Director, Breck Eisner, resorts to confusing camera shots and shocks to keep us hooked resulting in many muddled scenes. Eisner's attempt at artistic shots from unusual points of view ends in a visual feast that is nothing but a nauseous take-away you may regret later. And upon leaving the cinema, you can feel just like that; the sense of wasting money on a film that you probably could have written yourself. This being said the film does scare you which I suppose on some levels is the point. The film has a good knack for building up the right amount of tension to terrify you witless when something leaps at the screen. If you are looking for that kind of thrill without a complicated plot then The Crazies is just for you. Overall, The Crazies is no Oscar winner and that's OK but for a struggling genre all the audience craves is more originality. Perhaps if they left the original alone it would have that fresher feel to it. In the end, it is a predictable film trying to shock, scare and surprise you but instead leaves you with a boredom that would drive anyone crazy.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Having not seen the original film and lacking in sufficient background knowledge in order to know what to expect from 'The Crazies', I was most surprised to discover that it is a relentlessly tense feature, benefiting from clever direction and realistic characters portrayed by a talented cast that take to their roles seriously.Telling the story of a town overwhelmed by mayhem following the contamination of their water supply, 'The Crazies' rises above typical genre fare with consistently fresh scenarios throughout its running time, gradually unfolding the story for the group of survivors to piece together along with the audience, adding an element of mystery to the proceedings. 		<date>2010-07-15</date>
		<username>Spikeopath</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Boo-yah, indeed.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Directed by Breck Eisner and written by Scott Kosar &amp; Ray Wright, The Crazies (2010) is a remake of the 1973 film directed by George A. Romero. It stars Timothy Olyphant, Rhada Mitchell and Joe Anderson. The plot sees a toxic spill into a small American town's water system turn some of the locals into marauding maniacs.Eisner's movie is that rare old thing these days, that of the horror remake that greatly improves on the original. That might annoy some Romero purists, but the truth is is that his original film really isn't that great to begin with. Thus it's ripe for a remake, whilst acknowledging that a certain weariness creeps in to our thoughts at the seemingly never ending line of horror remakes churned out by a Hollywood running out of ideas. Hell I will even venture that we have seen all this before, nothing in this Crazies will have the horror faithful rushing out to tell their buddies about some overtly cranial splendour piece they have just watched. But this is a very effective horror piece, taut and tense at times, at others sick and splendidly disgusting. Eisner may not be a Craven, or for sure no Romero, but he executes the material with gusto and shows a knack for knowing how to make the material work.The film is structured over three parts - - character formations in the little town of the delightfully small Americana sounding Ogden Marsh, which leads into the infected going doolally-the army attempting to get things under control-and then the fall out as our brave survivors, erm, try to survive and make sense of what is happening. Eisner and his writers even get away with not fleshing out the principal characters. We know Olyphant's Sheriff is a toughie, and that his pregnant wife, Mitchell, is equally resourceful, while the deputy played by the film's standout performer, Joe Anderson, we know is loyal and sharp with a rifle. Who cares about flesh on these bones, let the crazies after them and see how they cope! Where the writers score good points is with the portrait of a world losing its humanity. The sick are rounded up and contained, nobody cares enough to try and help them, while those sent to restore order, to protect the people, are as dangerous as those bleeding from the eyes and ears. Madness everywhere.With memorable blood pumping scenes, bona fide suspense and metaphorical smarts in the writing, this is one damn fine remake shocker. 7.5/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2010-03-13</date>
		<username>MisterWhiplash</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>a solid retelling</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The original Crazies by George Romero was what should be called a "drive-in classic". Meaning it's not the kind of high art that should be reserved for other Romero films like Dawn of the Dead or Martin. This is a slam-bang action picture with a sharp political twist. Some of it is rough as a film, but it works more often than not. The director of this remake/re-imagining respects the integrity of Romero's message, but makes a film here that is solid on its own terms. It's a more than competently made "zombie" movie, though more like an infected-victim case where a toxin is released on a public in a small Iowa town, and the horror ensues when the few people who haven't become infected (either you do or you don't) have to escape the blockade put up by the military.It's as simple as that really, though along the way, as in any decent 'zombie' movie, our characters are just sharply drawn enough to know where they are at in an evolving sense in the story. This doesn't mean they stay exactly the same, nor do they change too radically one scene to another. One of the creative things Eisner does in his Crazies, as opposed to Romero's film, is to focus entirely on those trying to survive and escape, in this case a Sheriff, his wife, and his deputy, plus another girl. The deputy character, played by Joe Anderson (an actor to watch for sure), starts out like a capable bad-ass sidekick, a guy who can take out a nasty with his rifle and be able to keep his head. But at some point he just starts to lose it - maybe it's the sense of doom, or that he may just be getting sick- and it creates a lot of tension between the characters. It's also always believable because Eisner doesn't play it for laughs: this is, akin to Romero, who also executive produces, a serious, bloody horror movie.It doesn't mean every step is successful, or that the script shows the military as anything except ruthless and (mostly) faceless. Which is fine, since it gives a striking message in our current world, of how a military would go at a quarantine of an entire town (chaos ensues of course), and it also gives a strong parallel to the message of Romero's film, which was an Vietnam allegory. But certain moments are really shocking, even if we feel like we've seen them before. And there's one sequence particularly that is something of a mini-masterwork of suspense and horror. Often Eisner's The Crazies will try to shock with the JUMP scare (you know the kind, the cat lacking here thank goodness), and it more or less is passable. But at a scene where the characters hide from the military helicopter in a car-wash... and then the car-wash starts, and the Crazies come out of the wash and bubbles and machinery to go after our heroes, is harrowing. That, plus how the scene is resolved, in a very sharp one-TWO curve from laugh to total shock, is extraordinary.If the film has more moments like that it would be the smash horror film of the year. As it stands it's mostly just a good genre pic, respectful of the source, with a few individual touches. It can hold its own with the Zack Snyder Dawn of the Dead, for example.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Crazy film...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A remake that's actually better than the original, (it took a lot to write that) and for anyone who hasn't seen George A Romero's original, you'll come from the cinema feeling very satisfied indeed. Of course, Romero's 1973 original is the inspiration for this tongue-in-cheek horror remake, but it scores on so many levels.With efficient and classic genre thrills, the plot slowly evolves into a somewhat satisfying thrill ride of action, blood, and shocks - lots and lots of shocks, which may not actually shock people anymore because they know what's coming. OK, this is unfortunately the case, and some parts are as predictable as your average horror flick.Like most horror movies, the film relies on the ability to turn from one tense moment to the other, and director Breck Eisner concentrates mainly on the entertainment value, allowing his actors to have some fun. It may not be as crazy and mad as the original, but isn't that the point in remakes, to be slightly different? Very well produced, with a sturdy cast and director, The Crazies is easily the best remake of the last few years.Yes, we may have seen it all before, but Eisner has outdone himself here, and is easily his best work, and a film that all horror remakes should take note of. Highly recommended.</fullReview>

		<date>2012-10-20</date>
		<username>Scott LeBrun (Hey_Sweden)</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Solid 21st century version of the George A. Romero tale.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The 2010 version of Romero's "The Crazies" may not offer its audience material that they haven't seen before, but the good news is that it also rarely gives them a chance to breathe, delivering quite a bit of action and becoming genuinely exciting at times. The story, for the uninitiated, deals with a biological weapon that infects the water supply of a small town named Ogden Marsh, and drives most of the locals insane with homicidal rage. Low key sheriff David (Timothy Olyphant) and his doctor wife Judy (Radha Mitchell) hook up with other survivors to try to make their way to safety, while the military swoops in to try to contain the situation and this little section of the country turns into Hell on Earth. If you've seen movies such as the original "The Crazies", or Cronenberg's "Rabid", or Hollywood fare like "Outbreak", you pretty much know what to expect, but the characters are likable and their situation believable. What director Breck Eisner and company do is sweep the viewer along with the characters as they portray an event that basically snowballs way out of control. It begins quietly, as these things so often do, with a farmer interrupting a local ball game by walking onto the field with gun in hand. Some fine and chilling moments are created as the filmmakers show their willingness to treat most of their characters as fair game. Of course, there are also some fun gore moments, such as David managing to make use of a knife that's actually been rammed through his hand. All makeup and visual effects and stunts are well done, but what really drives the movie is the way that it shows the toll taken on our main characters. Romero's film showed the perspectives of both the protagonists and the military men in charge, while this film basically focuses on one group. Olyphant and the radiant Mitchell are fine as always, heading up a cast that also features Joe Anderson as Olyphants' deputy, Danielle Panabaker as a local gal, and character players such as John Aylward, Glenn Morshower, Larry Cedar, and Gregory Sporleder. Be sure to look for the cameo by original "Crazies" actress Lynn Lowry, as a lady on a bicycle; the only thing is that she doesn't get a close-up so that we know that it's her. Still, it's a nice touch, as it so often is when actors from an original appear in a remake, and the film moves along so well that it doesn't seem as long as it is. In the end, this is an entertaining film if not an innovative one, and that's what really matters, so horror &amp; action fans should have a good enough time with it. Seven out of 10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Another top notch horror remake that fills every need</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I have not seen the original film created by Zombie Master George Romero, in fact when I went in to see The Crazies I didn't even have the knowledge that this was a remake at all but even if I had known it wouldn't have swayed my opinions because I for one have loved the recent onslaught of horror film remakes. The Hills Have Eyes, The Ring, Nightmare on Elm Street, The Amityville Horror and so on. The Crazies is the perfect Stephen King-esquire Zombie flick that keeps you peeled to the screen from beginning to end. All the elements are firmly in place small town, mysterious outbreak, solid leading heroes and a great story. The Zombie angle can always seem predictable and overdone but The Crazies keeps the story fresh and original. The shocking aspect of the Zombie craze including the neighbours that are very close watching each other murder people in horrifying and insane ways. From the intensity of a well known neighbour stumbling into a baseball field with a shotgun to escaping from their small town this one will leave you literally on the edge of your seat.I have become a huge fan of Timothy Olyphant who I am convinced will continue to rise in stardom. He has amazing screen chemistry, he's always very versatile, and carries a film flawlessly. He proves that in The Crazies as he plays the cowboy like hero David. Radha Mitchell is also a terrific actress. She has a lot of terrific roles under her belt. Her and Olyphant have great chemistry together. She is always the perfect scream queen being a strong female lead and still show some vulnerability (see her in Silent Hill.) Joe Anderson is amazing as Olyphant's deputy. He's the good guy, but a bit of anti-hero and just fun to watch. He seems so naive and yet really has some strength to his character. I have become a fan of Danielle Panabaker ever since she played James Woods' daughter in Shark. She's cute, and spunky but can still be a very adult character. She good chemistry with the rest of the cast and its fun to see her in this role. The rest of the supporting cast are excellent but it really is focused entirely on the four of them.I think director Breck Eisner has a lot of promising films ahead of him especially now that he's connected to the Escape from New York remake. He really does well commanding the cast and the setting for The Crazies is absolutely perfect. He does the perfect mix of horror, thriller, zombie and even a touch of drama to the whole idea. I don't think its any coincidence that lead writer Scott Kosar has written some truly brilliant films and remakes that I previously mentioned. He penned the amazing Amityville Horror remake, Texas Chainsaw Massacre remake and the unheard of gem The Machinist. Some will of course call The Crazies terrible and everything else because its not the picture perfect Hollywood critics choice but for the real movie goers looking for true entertainment and if you're a horror buff you won't regret a single minute of The Crazies. 9/10</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Yes, man!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When I was a child, my mother would take me through the automatic car wash at a BP station near our home. One of my favorite parts was when the windshield would be covered in a layer of white soap. Eventually, there was a thin line of yellow, red, and blue soap added on top of the white one. Mother said it was a marketing trick to encourage kids to pester their folks for another visit. I loved the anticipatory feeling of waiting for the process to begin and then seeing the cloth flaps moving over our car. In the modern version of The Crazies, characters are fleeing from zombie-like sick people and the US military when they attempt to hide in an automatic car wash. They trigger the device and are suddenly obscured under a layer of rainbow soaps and cloth flaps. Their assailants are still outside and begin tearing into the car. After the car wash ends, we see the sick people, just before a bomb destroys the car (without the heroes in it).Those moments of very real fear from commonplace experiences give the new Crazies a more personalized feel than Romero's version. Director Breck Eisner (Sahara) places several cheap "boo" moments like that into this movie. Either he was clever enough to realize this story lacks the capacity to be great or he simply adhered to common horror clichés. I find the ending result to be a satisfying substitution for the seriousness and contrivance of the original. This movie is at least entertaining.Like the first film, a virus has been released into the water supply of a small town. It causes people to go crazy (get it?) and become great horror movie antagonists. It starts at a baseball game and soon nearly the entire population is under its effect. The US military sweeps up everyone and places them in a concentration camp adapted from a high school. The local sheriff, eluding capture, breaks in with his deputy to save his wife. The three of them later encounter a teenage couple and attempt to escape. The teenagers die and the deputy sacrifices himself to the military so the sheriff and his wife can escape. After a run in with diseased rednecks, the two of them arrive at a larger town that will now have to be quarantined.This may have been the type of movie Romero was trying to make. There is a lot of interaction between the crazies and heroes this time. No references are made to the government. The military's only storytelling purpose is to provide a faux feeling of terror whenever there are no crazies are around. Romero could have streamlined this disjointedness into a consistently scary atmosphere if his distributors had let him. Even though he didn't direct it, his trademarked anti-military message is still there. It's much less explicit than previously. Instead of seeing a government official say, "we have to stop the spread of this disease," a bomb is dropped on the town near the end. Eisner portrays the military as a whole as brutal, but spares individual soldiers from criticism. Like the cheapie scares, it's a clear cop-out. But in a remake, does that matter? Recommended</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>You would be crazy to miss it.......If you like survival horror</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Improving on the original Romero "classic" (as some call it) is not hard. The original had a shoe-string budget which-combined with poor acting- gave it an almost most 70's porn film production value. That aside the central premise is solid and this remake executes it well.An army bio-weapon, code named Trixie, is accidentally dumped into a small everyday Americana white picket-fenced homes in a farm town's water supply. Once ingested Trixie sets off a "viral" madness which grows exponentially out of control....The thunder has been stolen from this concept in recent years- the "28 days/weeks double being the most significant- but there is no use in complaining about that when you have flogged to death horror franchises and their rebooted efforts on the horizon every other month! Jason, Michael and Freddy please take note.....The film focuses as much on the military containment efforts- and the "clear-up" process which has shades of the Third Reich's final solution. These elements are more emotionally affecting than the well made horror action set-pieces, as the real horror is being treated like cattle by your own; ring-fenced with razor wire and bar-coded without explanation.What would have improved the film? A little more focus on the initial townsfolk, a flamboyant one take tracking camera shot of various acts of murder, madness and odd behaviour in the town- weaving through homes, back out into the street etc, just for a minute or two to give it scope. Another element which is played on, but not fully examined is "Do I have the virus or do you?" paranoia. This could have created a truly unsettling atmosphere and racked up the tension even more.One missed opportunity: I have an indeliable memory of a scene from the original. Although this version is flawed, it still had one of Romero's ace scenes in it- Picture if you will.... The view from inside the gas mask of a soldier who finds one of the locals in the infected town; a little old lady, smiling-rocking back and forth in her chair. The soldier is concerned, urging her to come with him and leave the house. She gets up- seemingly in total compliance- &amp; whilst still smiling, drives the knitting needles into the soldier! Now that could have been left in as a homage to the original. The ending of this remake has no real surprises, left open for an unnecessary sequel, but this film is a decent afternoon's popcorn entertainment horror flick.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Better than the original? Don't be crazy!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When Zack Snyder presented us with with his re-imagining of George Romero's Dawn of the Dead back in '04, he reminded many horror fans that, in the right hands, remakes can be surprisingly good (it also made Romero himself hot property again, although old George has done plenty to rectify that in recent years).Director Breck Eisner clearly intends to try and pull off the same trick with his updated version of Romero's 'lesser classic' The Crazies, which boasts equally slick production values, also features a talented but relatively unknown cast, and similarly concentrates more on action and shock horror than its comparatively subdued predecessor. And for a while, it looks as though he might succeed.Unfortunately, after a well executed first half during which Eisner carefully establishes his characters and gradually reveals their terrifying predicament, the story has very few places left to go; this leaves the director desperately trying to pad out his film to a decent running time before delivering the inevitable explosive finale. The result is a fairly fun but logic-free effort that offers one or two well handled scenes of gory mayhem, but also plenty of plot-holes, and quite a few moments of sheer inanity.Unforgivably, Eisner also botches the film's big revelation?the real secret behind the film's horrible epidemic?by having it blurted out in seconds by a hastily introduced character, before quickly returning to the action. Well, what did I really expect from the man who directed Sahara? I suppose I should be grateful for the fact that the film was any where near as entertaining as it actually was.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The Crazies</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The sheriff of Ogden Marsh, Iowa, finds his town overrun by the military when the citizens turn into psychopaths after drinking the water supply infected by a biological weapon called Trixie, caused by a downed plane which crashed in a lake. Sheriff David Dutton(Timothy Olyphant)goes back for his wife, Judy(Sasha Mitchell) after she is pulled away from him, separated because she was running a fever(she is pregnant), quarantined and left strapped to a gurney along with local girl, Becca(Danielle Panabaker)when the crazies break from a stronghold, executed by soldiers. Dutton, along with trusty deputy, Russell Clank(Joe Anderson, in a star making performance), will hunt for Judy, hoping to find her before danger strikes. Not only will they have to avoid the infected but the military as well, who seem to now be operating by orders to kill the entire town. Hoping to evade every threat they come across, on foot unless they are to find a vehicle not ruined purposely by the military, David, Judy, Russell, and Becca will try their very level best to get out of dodge, attempting to flee the perimeter.Was this one intense! I often applaud a movie that has me biting my nails..a nail biter to me is accomplished when it draws you in through the perilous situations which plague characters which I care for. It's inevitable that we compare one film to another, the original to it's "re-invisioning", and I admit, being an absolute admirer of Romero's critically maligned classic(it seems that even Romero faithful question "The Crazies" worth, sad to say), I think that director Breck Eisner's version has better leads..frankly, I found the leads in Romero's movie rather colorless, for the most part. There are some nice homages to Romero's original which I credit Eisner and the filmmakers for such as the horrifying sequence involving the father setting ablaze his house with his wife and kid he locked in a closet trapped inside, and an amusing cameo from Lynn Lowry as a woman singing a tune while riding her bicycle in town! Potent suspense sequences galore..Judy and Becca are strapped to a gurney as the man they once knew as a principal is stabbing other bodies with a pitchfork closing in on them, David having to shoot a farmer who was toting a shotgun during a baseball game in front of the attending crowd, David is then attacked by that farmer's infected wife and son while Judy is tied to a chair, the truck stop finale as David and Judy must defend themselves against savage hunters afflicted with virus, and an attack on David, Judy, Russell, and Becca while their car is stuck in a car wash machine. In this remake, we not only see mental deterioration and eventual psychopathic lapses into violence, but hideous physiological effects as well. Like in the original, the virus is named Trixie and the cause remains identical, a plane crashes into a lake, carrying a biological agent to poison an American enemies' city, entering accidentally into the water of our own people. I liked the decision in this movie to focus the central plot around the sheriff and his deputy. Russell saves David's life like three different times, and remains a vital emotional arc in the movie as he discovers he might be infected as he becomes more paranoid, using his gun when not needed, and behaving irrationally. One of the more shocking scenes involves Becca having to watch helplessly as her boyfriend and his mom are executed by soldiers, ordered, it seems, by their superiors, to eliminate anyone in the city. Another terrible discovery at the truck stop eerily reminds us of the holocaust, a subtext that is quite disturbing, not to mention the gathering of the citizens by the military as if they were sheep(and, again, we see the soldiers gunning them down as they herd in fear, even those who haven't succumbed to the infection)to the slaughter. The military, unlike Romero's, remain faceless, hiding behind face masks and bio-gear, chemical suits and machine guns. Only two specific military types are unmasked because they service the movie as exposition of the plot. I will warn you that the film uses loud sound effects to amplify jump scares, but I think the sparse musical score, which does it's job, I felt, at mounting unease and dread, and the use of silence as characters move about outside and throughout whatever building they might be at at any given moment, not knowing where an infected lunatic might be lurking, can be positively spine-tingling.I do think there logical lapses from time to time with moments where I assume viewers will be eye-rolling, whispering to themselves, "Yeah, right." What I mean by that is there are plenty of times where characters escape the jaws of death in the nick of time, our heroes are able to avoid capture, walking freely in the open despite the knowledge that the military is round abouts. No doubt about it, the outlook is grim from the opening shot of the town of Ogden Marsh desolate and burning with the sound of flames never more wrenching. Finally, Olyphant graduates into the lead role with fine support from Mitchell as his concerned/worried pregnant wife, watching everyone they've ever knew killed or turning into monster. Certainly one of the better remakes I've seen in recent memory because it knows how to push your buttons..or it certainly did mine!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>'The Crazies' while interesting, is mostly by-the-numbers</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A remake of George Romero's 1973 film, The Crazies, Timothy Olyphant (Hitman) plays Dave Dutton, a sheriff in a small town that has become infected by a virus causing the citizens to start killing each other. Dave finds out that this was probably due to the water supply being tainted by the government's accidental chemical spill. He and his doctor wife Judy (Radha Mitchell) and two friends are soon chased by the crazies (the infected) as well as the military who are trying to eradicate all the infected (and possibly the uninfected).Now, I have not seen the original, so I can't say whether this film is a better film than that one or whether it's wise even to use the earlier film as a standard to gauge any film. I will say that I'll review it as I always do (as it should be) based on my personal experience. With that said, I'll first say I do enjoy sci-fi horror much more than fantasy horror or slashers--they often usually have some underlying theme somewhere. With this film, there are certainly themes regarding the dangers of the "big brother" government coverups.This film, in essence, is a zombie film, although they aren't technically zombies. Much like 28 Days Later, they are like viral zombies (because it involves a virus), generally--they're still people, but not quite themselves. What makes them dangerous is that they want to kill normal people. Unfortunately, we're not told in detail exactly how this virus works that makes them want to kill. Does it make people aggressive and rabid like 28 Days Later? Not really--many of these guys seem rather calculated and well, slow, most of the time. For me, them being "crazy" didn't seem like a strong enough reason. After all, these aren't cannibals like zombies that has a hunger nor a built-in need to assimilate the population, like pod people. These guys generally just want to kill. If new people do get infected by the virus, it's mostly by accident, not because these guys bite. Regular zombies are scarier than these "crazies" because they play with our fears of the animalistic, killer nature. These guys, however, while they do use tools, just don't elicit that feeling of urgent threat that zombies give.On the other hand, one may argue that the real enemy is the government. Not only do the protagonists have to deal with the crazies, they have to deal with the military trying to kill them. This aspect makes the film feel more like a thriller than a horror film.I felt director Breck Eisner's approach to this film was quite by-the-numbers. There aren't anything I haven't seen before and I found many scenes tobe mostly predictable (if you've seen films of this ilk). I did, though, enjoy the tense set up at the beginning involving the sheriff'sstand-off with a so-called "town drunk." Mostly, I felt rest of the film relied too much on jump scenes punctuated by loud tunes, a overused gimmick seen in many modern B-movie horror flicks. There's plenty of close-up shots and shaky camera. With that said, there are some fun scenes, such as the car-wash scene and the ending--they were somewhat different. I wished the film worked more on the feeling of atmosphere. For the squeamish, there are plenty of gore and many corpses in this film.Timothy Olyphant as the sheriff remains consistently likable, despite the fact that he doesn't have any deep moments that push him beyond the usual confines of the genre. Radha Mitchell (Surrogates) comes off very natural and sympathetic as his pregnant wife. True, the dialogue could've done more with her (especially since she's a doctor and a doctor in a horror flick could be used to great effect, I would think), but she works well with what she's given. The character interactions are usually fun and Joe Anderson as the deputy add much needed humor and some danger.Overall, while I was entertained, many scenes felt a little repetitive and excitement or actual creepiness factor wasn't particularly high for me. Whether it's more a homage or simply lack of innovation, the movie felt quite by-the-numbers in its execution. I suppose it didn't help that the "crazies" felt like toned-down zombies to me. Interesting ending, though. I give The Crazies ** ? out of **** stars.You can find my updates of movie reviews on http://twitter.com/d_art</fullReview>

		<username>Jonny_Numb</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>The Crazies Will Inherit the Earth</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I love the social-commentary-infused horror films of George Romero as much as any other genre loyalist, but I think most would forgive my dissent toward his 1973 effort, "The Crazies": regarding the fallout of a biological warfare agent accidentally released in a small Pennsylvania town that reduces the residents to screw-loose murderers, the film was a lethargic mix of preachy politics and over-the-top gore that ultimately did little more than affirm that, yes, guys in HazMat suits ARE pretty darned creepy. The long-gestating remake of "The Crazies" (director Brad "Session 9" Anderson was once attached to direct) is one genre revision I have actually been looking forward to. The good news? The filmmakers didn't screw it up. Taking the husk of Romero's original plot and dissolving much of the heavy-handed sermonizing, Scott ("The Machinist") Kosar's script instead focuses on an intimate group of characters (including Timothy Olyphant and Radha Mitchell) facing a surreal, 48-hour societal breakdown, and the measures they resort to in order to survive. Kosar's script is a model of genre economy: he keeps the characters realistic, the locations authentic, the situations genuinely terrifying, and the tone refreshingly barren of ironic, tongue-in-cheek humor. As I watched "The Crazies," fond memories of John Erick Dowdle's similarly-themed "Quarantine" came to mind; while yet another remake (of the well-regarded Spanish shocker "(REC)"), it transcended its parent by never allowing its tone to be anything less than urgent, and keeping the story moving at a breakneck pace. Thanks to the surprisingly confident direction of Breck (son of Michael) Eisner, the transition from script to screen is frequently astonishing: while the opening minutes of the film are somewhat wobbly (with moments of bad acting and an unfortunate prelude scored to Johnny Cash), "The Crazies" hits its stride quickly, presenting a group of characters worth rooting for and a sense of small-town familiarity that is only heightened (and made all the more terrifying) by the use of an earth-tone color scheme and gritty digital video. For most of its running time, "The Crazies" is a remake to be reckoned with, using a bigger budget and improved FX to diligently cement its impact: too bad the post-production, "sequel much?" final shot cheapens some of what has gone on before.6.5 out of 10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Crazily Good!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Crazies is a remake of George A. Romero's 1973 film of the same name. The story revolves around a town whose water supply has been poisoned by an mysterious and unknown toxin.To be honest there's nothing really new here, but that really doesn't matter. The Crazies is made in such a way that not many other horror movies are. Rather than just throwing random and senseless violence at you, director Breck Eisner actually builds up suspense instead. It works brilliantly and it's a refreshing change from other recent horrors that are basically just splatter-fests.Although this story has been done before in movies such as 28 Days Later and, to some extent, Cabin Fever, this movie plays it out incredibly well. They're not conventional looking or acting zombies but they're still pretty menacing and amazingly fierce.I'd recommend The Crazies to any horror fan who wants something a little bit different than what's currently out there. It really is a scarily great movie.http://ukmore.tk/</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A step up from the original, but still dull overall</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When I first saw the original George Romero film The Crazies a few years ago, my first thought after it was over was that it was a pretty great concept that wasn't executed the right way. That's actually my only memory regarding the original film since it wasn't very memorable and not even up to par with Romero's mediocre films. Before news hit that the film was being remade, the thought crossed my mind that a remake could certainly benefit this film. Lo and behold, the film has been remade and is in theaters now.The 2010 remake of The Crazies can be considered an upgrade from the original film. Is it an original concept? No, not really. But when dealing with a remake, aiming for an original concept is kind of silly since it was already done at least once in the original film it's based off of. The Crazies actually felt a lot like if 28 Weeks Later and The Hills Have Eyes (2006) were blended together to make one film. The military being heavily involved and trying to contain the outbreak is what made me think of 28 Weeks Later. The "crazies" themselves and the ending were reminiscent to the last scene at the end of The Hills Have Eyes. Actually, Neil Marshall's Doomsday comes to mind as well. The car wash scene instantly made me think of last year's The Final Destination, which I absolutely hated. Luckily, this car wash was a bit more exciting and the payoff was more satisfying. I hate doing comparisons to other films while thinking of this one, but that was one of the things that irked me about the film. It seemed like most things that occurred throughout the duration of the film were reminiscent of others. Most films do that, but usually have a few distinguishing scenes of their own. A remake, if done properly, should remind you of the original film while introducing a few new elements that stand alone. Originality goes a long way in this medium these days.The pitchfork scene is the main reason to see this film. I've always wondered why pitchforks haven't been used more often in horror films. The movie poster is a huge tease to that scene and it is easily the highlight. Timothy Olyphant in the lead role is another good reason to see this. Olyphant seems to be the type of actor that is skilled enough to get bigger roles, but either picks the wrong parts or is looked over by the studios. He at least had the opportunity to shine in last year's A Perfect Getaway and again here.While The Crazies is an improvement over the original film, it still seems to be lacking something to make it more memorable and is a bit too predictable. There are quite a few interesting kills (the way the lighter comes into play isn't too shabby) and the make-up and CG effects are more than satisfactory, but it often felt like the film borrowed ideas from other horrors and thrillers from the past few years rather than providing something a bit more creative. The Crazies falls a bit flat overall, but is still worth checking out if you're a die hard horror fan.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Did Peter call?......</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After a slew of well known horror movies being remade in an awful way (too many to mention, we all know the culprits....Hello PG-13!!!) comes a little known seventies paranoia movie, which is surprisingly good and rather effective.The town of Ogden Marsh is experiencing some of the town folk, going a little bit....Crazy. Just who are the titular characters. On first viewing, it's simple to label the infected as the titular characters. 		<username>zardoz-13</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A Solid Remake of a Classic Chilller</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A catastrophic quandary and several creepy scenes make this ghoulish remake of director George A. Romeo's 1973 classic scary movie "The Crazies" worth watching if you can handle horror movies. Mind you, this $20 million, R-rated quasi-zombie epic boasts just enough gore to qualify for its R-rating. Nevertheless, "Sahara" director Breck Eisner relies on omission during some macabre acts of violence to prompt you to use your own imagination for greater horrific effect than any computer generated imagery. Indeed, this bizarre movie contains suspenseful situations and chilling characters that may have you poised you on the edge of your seat. "The Crazies" cuts its characters no slack, and omnipotent forces are at work here that our heroes cannot comprehend. "Amityville Horror" remake scribe Scott Kosar and "Pulse" writer Ray Wright deliberately have confined the scope of the action to a quartet of characters: a sheriff, his doctor wife, his resourceful deputy, and her young female assistant. Not only does this allow Eisner to drum up a palatable sense of claustrophobia, but it also enables him to breed a queasy paranoia. Eisner maintains the tension throughout without slackening the momentum in this entertaining but sinister melodrama. Moreover, "The Crazies" depicts the dark side of democracy, particularly the wholesale cover-up that the government orchestrates to wipe out any trace of its incompetence after an accident occurs. Not only do our heroes and heroines find themselves trapped when regular Army troops try to exterminate them with extreme prejudice, but they also must contend with their own insane kind that have an urge to kill. Audaciously enough, Eisner and his scenarists shun both incidental humor as well as comic relief characters so that "The Crazies" never turns into a parody.Everybody in a small rural Iowa farming town, with a population of less than 1300 people, suddenly goes berserk in the spring for no apparent reason. Pierce County Sheriff David Dutton (Timothy Olyphant of "Hitman") is baffled beyond description about why lunacy is the rage. "The Crazies" opens with Ogden Marsh, Iowa, ablaze in a sea of flames. The story begins two days before this quiet Midwestern town erupted into a conflagration. David attends the first high school baseball game of the season with his Deputy Russell Clank (Joe Anderson of "Amelia") when the town drunk shows up in the outfield. David spots the shotgun that Rory Hamill (Mike Hickman of "The Lena Baker Story") has cradled in his hands and hustles across the infield to confront Rory. Meanwhile, Russell clears the teenagers off the baseball diamond. David tries to reason with Rory, but the disturbed man brandishes his shotgun. David drops Rory with one shot. Later, Deardra Farnum (Christie Lynn Smith of "Woman on Top") takes her husband, Bill Farnum (Brett Rickaby of "The Scout"), into the local family medical clinic. David's wife Judy (Radha Mitchell of "High Art") runs the clinic. Judy examines Bill but finds nothing wrong with him. Bill goes berserk that night locks Deardra and son in an upstairs closet and then incinerates the house. David locks up Bill and the psycho stands in his jail cell like a statue for hours until he collapses his face changes colors and his blood veins pop out. Eventually, David visits the town water department superintendent because our hero suspects something might have gotten into the drinking water.Before they realize what has happened, David and Judy find themselves rounded up by U.S. Army soldiers wearing in gas masks and toting assault rifles and hauled off with Russell in a bus to a containment unit. David struggles without success to find somebody in authority with whom he can reason. Eventually, the troops in their protective uniforms separate Judy and David. Judy and David aren't apart for long because other hysterical Ogden Marsh citizens go on the rampage and careen a truck into the Army's containment camp. While David and Russell escape and arm themselves, the military beats a retreat. They leave poor Judy strapped down helplessly to a gurney with no way to escape. They abandon many others like Judy at the local high school when the alarm sounds about the on-slaught of insane citizens. The Army scrambles aloft in their helicopters. Judy lies horror-stricken on her back when her friend, high school Principal Ben Sandborn (Larry Cedar of "Kiss the Girls"), wanders into the area dragging a pitchfork and looking like a nightmare. The tines of the pitchfork screech against the floor as Ben tours the restrained bodies. Spotting Judy, he approaches our heroine, and hoists his pitchfork to impale her like his two previous victims.Olyphant registers solidly in one of his better performances as the beleaguered but sympathetic hero. Cast as the even-handed Pierce County Sheriff David Dutton, the likable Olyphant encounters mayhem and murder like never before during a three day period, while Radha Mitchell appears just as convincing as his beleaguered wife in the early stages of a pregnancy. She also serves as the town doctor so that she isn't entirely out of the loop with regard to the hysteria afflicting her small town. Jon Anderson is especially good as Sheriff Dutton's deputy, who saves his life three times before he suffers from the biochemicals. Director Eisner stages the action with aplomb. Eisner breathes new life into the conventional scene when the hero has trouble getting his vehicle to crank up while the villains approach him. Although he falls back on standard horror movie tactics with an occasional sudden jolt, "The Crazies" is pretty straightforward without being sickening. Eisner and his writers do an excellent job of foreshadowing two subplots that they pay off in subsequent scenes. The knife-in-the-hand scene is one of the best. Altogether, "The Crazies" doesn't meet the criteria of a stupid horror movie with characters doing cretinous deeds to avoid death.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Fear Thy Neighbor</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A reinvention loosely based upon the George Romero classic of the same name, THE CRAZIES is about the inhabitants of a small Kansas town suddenly plagued by insanity and then death after a mysterious toxin contaminates their water supply.I know you've seen it before: A small town is infected and eventually taken over by zombies, crazy people, the government, or any combination of these forces. A few survivors stick with each other in order to survive through the chaos, or, in this case, madness, around them. However, you'd be crazy to judge a film by whether or not the plot is original because look at AVATAR.I like zombie/infection/virus movies, because they are usually entertaining and thrilling to watch, but I have not seen the original film by Romero yet, so I cannot compare this remake with it. However, the film does stand up well in the wave of the many, many horror remakes that have been released. It's a good horror movie because it has one key ingredient that many horror movies fail to ever grasp: The suspense.This film manages to not overuse the cheap scare tactics, even though some are scattered here and there every now and then. There's also some genuine horror and tension here. There's one memorable sequence taking place in a car wash where I was on the edge of my seats. Almost everything in this film is done so well where an average horror movie wouldn't ever succeed in accomplishing half of it.Timothy Olyphant is another key ingredient to the film's success. Although he has been in many films before, this is his first starring role and he does a great job at it. Radha Mitchell is his on screen wife who also gives us a sympathetic role with some actual acting in her performance. Joe Anderson and Danielle Panabaker are the remaining two "main" actors here, the former giving a crazy but good performance while the latter gives us a generic whimpering teen.It's also good to know the craftsmanship also went to the look of the film as it did the script. I love the cinematography with its gracefulness, giving the film a stylish look and the soundtrack by Mark Isham is really heartfelt. And it's great to see director Breck Eisner get back to his feet after the box office bomb SAHARA. If you're wondering if the film has the social commentary contained in almost every George A. Romero film, you need not worry here. It's done in such a way the filmmakers did not push it down our throats.Overall, this is how every horror movies should be, let alone remakes, although they aren't, which still remains to us a great mystery. There are some great and ingenious sequences in here along with tension and suspense. The gore is actually used sparingly, although when it does get used, it's really nasty. It's just a really bloody horror flick with intelligence and good film-making. Films like THE CRAZIES give us hope to the slowly dying horror genre. Let's hope Hollywood makes more movies like this.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A predictable, but fun ride.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There isn't much good coming out of Hollywood horror these days. Most of the good horror films end up on the independent market or come in a foreign package, but even then, the list of good horror films these days is short and thin. So, it's a bit surprising to find myself saying the The Crazies is actually a decent bit of Hollywood horror. Albeit, it is a remake, but it runs more in the vein of the Dawn of the Dead remake than the Friday the 13th. The Crazies is pretty predictable and is fairly standard fare. Small town essentially does the zombie thing (they might as well be zombies, though seeing the people act crazy is kinda fun), there is a shoot- first, never answer questions military cover up, and our heroes face off against all said threats. Most horror film clichés are thrown in and there are very few surprises. Yet the film remains entertaining, partly due to the great cinematography, but mostly due to it's lead cast, predominantly Timothy Olyphant. He continues to prove that he is one of this generations best new character actors with another great role that will probably end up going mostly unnoticed. He plays the hero to a T, and does a great job every minute of it. Suffice to say, he is the most enjoyable aspect of this film. Rhada Mitchell also continues to prove herself as a fine actress who deserves more notice. She puts up a near equally good performance in her semi-heroine role, tackling some very physical aspects. The films real downfall is it's pacing issues. Its initial pace is pretty speedy, with the story moving through quite a bit within the first 50 minutes. After this mark, things start to slow down. There are some decent set pieces, including a great car wash scene and a decent truck stop scene, but I kept wondering if we had reached the climax already. I had to look at my watch a few times just to see if the film was too short or not. It also didn't help that most of the horror gimmicks, though somewhat effective, had been seen before. This also kind of slowed down the film as little.All in all, The Crazies is a good weekend at the cinema in a weekend of few notable movies. If you're looking for something more Hollywood and entertaining, The Crazies should suffice.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>"Next stop, Zombieland, Iowa!"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Just when you think you've seen enough survivalist zombie flicks, here comes another---and not surprisingly, an unassuming remake of George Romero's 1973 b-movie cult classic of the same name.This remake however, is quite a top-notch fright fest with just enough "WTF" ambiguity to satisfy hardcore sci-fi fans of the Comic-Con generation, and with enough blood and guts to satiate the average frat boy demographic who just can't get enough of the torture-porn horror movies of late. The "same-old, same-old" plot notwithstanding, the execution here is clever and inventive enough to generate genuine seat-sinking moments of dread and suspense. To use a well-tuned sports sedan as a metaphor---think, for example an early model Acura TSX--- it is by design a fun car to drive (action), with just the right amount of power (plot twists), useful gadgets (actors), standard features (CGI and makeup) and to top it off having all components and panels fitting together just right (editing and visuals). Relating further to fancier model cars with all the stupefying bells, whistles and 'bling' that elevate things to the "whoa, cool!" stratosphere, this one takes a conservative and rather defiant approach to a tired and true horror genre and makes it all fresh and alive. Well, at least for now.The cast is pitch-perfect. Timothy Olyphant has just enough aw-shucks good looks and bravado to make him both likable and believable as the small-town sheriff. Radha Mitchell, who was terrific although underutilized in a recent sci-fi flick called "Surrogates," has taken on the woman in peril role to new heights as the sheriff's pregnant wife. In what would otherwise be a thankless role, she does succeed in her extended freak and scream moments that would have made Jamie Lee Curtis proud! Like "28 Days Later" and even as far back as the original "The Hills Have Eyes," this remake of "The Crazies" is a winner in every way. Put it down as a purchase once it's released on home video, and display it proudly amongst the rest of your zombie and torture porn DVD collection!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Not Bad, but could've been better</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The remake of The Crazies was a good enough film for the most part. The acting was decent and the scares pretty good. The pacing was real good and keeps your interest throughout, but I think it needed a little more mounting tension. It starts out well, but becomes more of a shock fest near the end, and that takes away some of the movie's power.After a quick scene of a small town burning at the beginning, we are pointed to two days prior where a baseball game is being played. One of the local residents, looking very much off, walks onto the field with a shotgun, and the local sheriff (Timothy Olyphant) steps forward after telling everybody to take cover. Trouble ensues and suddenly a small once peaceful town is thrown into a nightmare that they can't escape. You see, something has contaminated the water supply, and it causes people to go insane and start killing one another. How this all comes about, I will not say, but the town sheriff, his pregnant wife (Radha Mitchell), the sheriff's deputy (Joe Anderson), and a town friend (Danielle Panabaker) try to survive and make their way to the next town in hopes that it is not infected, but things aren't that easy, as our four protagonists soon find out.Following in the footsteps of 28 Days later and 28 Weeks later, The Crazies is a movie about normal human beings becoming bloodthirsty maniacs after a virus has infected them. The Crazies is not on the same world scale as those first two movies, but the infected, just like those first two movies, are not really zombies at all, but just sick people. All three movies are metaphors on the real world and what it has become. Both 28 Days Later and 28 weeks later ratcheted the tension up and then hit us with the shocks. THe Crazies starts out in much the same vain but the tension is more like a whimper, and then the shocks happen. Of course this movie does contain a car wash scene that could put it right up there with the first two movies, but then little else happens to match that one scene.As I said, the acting is pretty good for a horror movie. You do care about what happens to our four heroes as they make their way through this nightmarish landscape. Some have said their wasn't much chemistry between the husband and wife, but when you're trying to survive, some of that marital affection might just get thrown out the window.My beef with the film was not the acting, nor the pacing, or even the gore and shocks which were very effective when used. What I felt after seeing this movie was that I was not scared enough, or even creep-ed out that much. Gore is good and all, as are simple shocks to jar you, but a good or great horror movie has to have more suspense. The Crazies didn't have enough of that. I will admit that it is better than most other horror movies put out recently, but I can't quite give it my recommendation.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A Rare Remake That's Better Than The Original</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The remake is one of the most dreaded things in horror today. With a few exceptions (like the "Dawn of the Dead" remake), most remakes tend to either a.) Add nothing new or exciting to the equation, or b.) Going the safe, PG-13 route. So when it was announced that Michael Eisner's son Breck was directing a remake of George A. Romero's 1973 film "The Crazies", I was more than a little skeptical. I mean, the original, while a good movie, isn't exactly one of Romero's best works, so the idea of remaking it was no skin off of my back. It was just the choice of director that irked me. Thankfully, Eisner got it right, not only doing a remake that improves upon the original, but also manages to be one of the much better horror remakes.In the town of Ogden Marsh, Iowa, a plane crash has caused a bio-weapon known as "Trixie" to leak into the water supply. Said chemical has a noticeable side effect-it causes people to turn insane and commit acts of violence and murder. So what does the government do? The send in the military to control the situation. This isn't exactly an easy epidemic to contain however, and sheriff David Dutton (Timothy Olyphant), his pregnant wife Judy (Radha Mitchell) and two others (Joe Anderson and Danielle Panabaker) soon realize that the so called "Crazies" might not be the biggest threat.Eisner's version of "The Crazies" has two things going for it that even Romero's original didn't-good performances and actual scares. Everyone involved-particularly Olyphant and Mitchell-does a fine job with the material, never going over the top or overacting, and helping add some weight and reality to the situation at hand. At the same time, this is a tense little movie, as Eisner pulls off several suspenseful set pieces that constantly get the job done effectively, the highlight being a scene in a car wash. Oh, and there's a really fun cameo appearance from the originals own Lynn Lowery, herself something of a cult icon.One thing this movie and the original get right is an intelligent social commentary on government incompetence. Government's all over the world have tried to deal with serious situations in unnecessarily violent manners, so the idea of the government violently mishandling a situation isn't the least bit shocking-look at things such as the Iraq War and especially Guantanamo Bay for example.In the end, "The Crazies" is the rare remake that get's the job done, and manages to actually improve on the original. It's surprising to see Hollywood of all things be the ones who got it right, but at least someone did.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Weaker than the original, but I still thought it was decent</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I seem to be one of the few people who really loves George A. Romero's original 1973 film The Crazies, but, I have to say, a remake wasn't a totally bad idea. The original is very well written and directed, but the awful acting is off-putting, and I understand why most people wouldn't care for it. I actually didn't think this remake improved much in that department. Timothy Olyphant and Radha Mitchell star as a married couple trying to get out of an Iowa town being torn to shreds by a mysterious virus and the invasion of the U.S. military. Joe Anderson and Danielle Panabaker accompany them. Anderson and Panabaker are decent (Panabaker is adorable), but Olyphant and Mitchell are low B-level stars and neither of them brings much to the table. I thought Olyphant was kind of bad, really. I mean, it's not the amateurish acting of the original, but it isn't much better. The film itself is pretty good for a modern horror film. It lacks the complexity of the original one. They drop the military and medical points of view entirely, and instead just focus on the fugitives trying to get outside the perimeter. That idea to focus the film is kind of a good idea (I would assume the people backing it didn't want to confuse a young audience with social commentary), but with a more singleminded focus, the film does become a tad repetitive. It is saved by a handful of excellent horror sequences. I loved the opening bit, with the crazed gunman walking onto a high school baseball field with a shotgun. And there's a sequence set in a carwash that will go down as one of the great horror sequences. Carwashes are so claustrophobic as it is, it just works brilliantly. This film is definitely worth a rental at least.</fullReview>

		<username>Rathko</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Great Production; Lousy Script</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm almost ashamed to say that I've never seen the Romero original, so I'm going into 'The Crazies' totally blind. On the whole, it's a better movie than many recent remakes. The generic script, riddled with genre clichés, is thankfully salvaged (in part) by director Breck Eisner. He has a visual flare and a sympathetic approach to characters and location that lifts the movie above its drive-in origins. The tension is built with some success in several scenes, though it's too-often cut short before it reaches nerve-wracking heights. The Crazies themselves are barely-glimpsed, and on the rare occasion we get to see them they are disappointing carbon copies of every madness-inducing virus victim thrown up in the last ten years. Though the film carries an R certificate, there's little on screen to warrant the rating, and the violence and brutality necessary to make these guys truly terrifying is sadly missing. The primary problem, however, is a screenplay that never manages to convey any real sense of urgency. Characters seem free to wander aimlessly at will and the Crazies are so small in number as to be easily avoided. Imagine '28 Days Later' with barely a dozen Infected in the city and you get the idea. As such, there's rarely any sense of immediate danger. The film is entertaining and Eisner and his cast can't be praised enough for their attempts to boost a dull screenplay into something of quality (hence my rating). It's just a shame that newcomers Overture Films seems to share the disregard that more established production companies have for horror and scupper their product from the outset by hiring hack writers.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>jgm2602</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Surprising...Tense and Gritty...Not Your Standard Horror Flic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I went into this movie with a group of friends, thinking this movie would be but another clichéd and dull horror film that the Hollywood machine has been churning out lately, but I am delighted to say that this movie, rather than disappoint, exceeded my expectations.Director Breck Eisner's The Crazies remains unlike so many horror flics in that a genuine tension pervades the entirety of the film. There is rarely a dull moment or slack in the tension. This tension exists largely because Eisner develops the main characters, the Sheriff (Olyphant), his wife Julie (Mitchell) and his deputy (Anderson), causing the viewer to care if they live or die. Additionally, the actors also surpass the acting echelon normally associated with a horror film.The refreshing aspect of this film remains its unpredictability. Unlike most horror movies, no nudity or sex pervades the film, implied or otherwise. Furthermore, the sequencing of who lives and who dies, how, when, and if they die, remains non-formulaic. In a normal horror movie, you might see death's predictable plan, laugh at the character's stupidity, and watch them die. However, The Crazies breaks this paradigm. Characters die or are threatened by death in unexpected scenes and methods. Even the foreshadowing occasionally misleads, creating a greater sense of tension.Regarding the film's gore, this film does not disappoint. There is a great deal of macabre imagery and gritty detail throughout the film, yet the extent of the gore does not feel gratuitous or ridiculous.The combination of these different elements coalesces to create great tension throughout the film. Although there are some moments of comic relief, the director does an excellent job of avoiding unwanted laughter during moments of suspense. During one particularly tense scene, I was actually inching up and backward in my seat, as if trying to scoot backward to escape the impending infliction that rushed toward one character (if you have seen the film you'll know which scene).Collectively, I would highly recommend this film to anyone looking for a good horror film. While this is not the type of film that will haunt you long after you've left the theater, it provides excellent tension throughout its duration. It's a roller-coaster ride; just enjoy it and go!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Solid but not outstanding. Gave me nightmares! :)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Crazies is a remake of a film that was once original, but these days zombie films are a dime a dozen. Does The Crazies stand out?I felt it to be a solid film but one that did not come across as overly original. However, just when I was thinking, "this is going to be pretty formulaic", there was a plot twist involving outsiders that changed the entire nature of the film. Unfortunately, this new premise was never explored or explained fully enough to build tension. If we don't know what the protagonists are fighting against, and therefore what their goal is, how much can we root for them?Although people have called this film a commentary on environmental damage and pollutants -- the thing that causes the Zombies -- isn't fleshed out enough to be a real talking point of the film. Anger and fear of the government don't constitute political commentary, I'm sorry. Also, there's confusion as to whether it's really a pollutant or more like a virus that is contagious. If it's contagious, it is a good thing that the protagonists want to escape the zombies and possibly infect the world?To its credit, there is plenty of action, and the action sequences play into the drama. There's some random violence, but most of the conflicts build character development and push the characters even further into terror and desperation. The film also avoids superhuman stunts. There's no MacGuyver or Keanu Reaves type in the film who knows exactly what to do and makes the film too campy to be scary.That being said, the action scenes aren't overly original. And the power of the big bang ending is wasted, neither being adequately built up nor adequately used as an obstacle to be overcome. The tension in the scene of crossing the highway is sort of wasted as well. And I do wish movieland people driving dangerously would fasten their seat belts. All in all, The Crazies is a solid film that really did give me a nightmare. I'll give it a respectable but not classic 7 out of 10.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Surely this should have been crazier?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Crazies" isn't just yet another remake of a classic horror gem, it's also yet another horror film in which the zombies aren't in fact zombies but people infected with a virus. Back in 1973, when the legendary George A. Romero thought it up for his movie, this was still an incredibly original twist, but before the remake reached us there also was "28 Days Later", "REC", "Ponytool", "Carriers", etc? Basically you could state that the "The Crazies" was ripped off multiple times before it got remade and hence this updated version is even more pointless and redundant than all the other remakes out there. Oh well, perhaps this is all just a little too skeptical and I nevertheless wanted to give "The Crazies" the benefit of the doubt when I went to see it at the Brussels International Festival of Fantastic Films, together with a theater full of wildly enthusiast horror fanatics. The film has quite a few things going for it, like a strong cast and the support of Romero as a producer, but I never at one point felt this was a good movie. There honestly isn't that much going on in "The Crazies", especially not in comparison to the original. That movie depicted mass hysteria and paranoia at its most intense. Romero confused even the viewer by not letting us know properly which characters were infected and which ones weren't. For all we know, they could all simply be trigger-happy Pennsylvanian hillbillies, and still most of movie's menacing atmosphere came from those anonymous but hyper-creepy military men dressed in white suits and their face covered by gas masks. This new version sadly just seems to revolve on one young couple, David and Judy Dutton and their unborn baby, trying to escape from their deadly infected hometown. He's the sheriff and she's the doctor of a little Iowa town called Ogden Marsh, when suddenly some of the friendly locals begin to behave like emotionless psychopaths that set fire to their own houses and show up at the Sunday morning baseball game with a shotgun in their hands. Next thing we know a couple of fishermen stumble upon a crashed down military aircraft in the swamp and suddenly the little town is overrun with soldiers that are gathering all the Ogden Marsh inhabitants to put under quarantine. David and a small group of survivors discover that the town's water supply got contaminated with a secret government supported virus called Trixie and that nobody has a proper idea of the exact impact and consequences. When they realize they can't save their fellow citizens, the only thing left for David and Judy to do is run for their lives. "The Crazies" is never boring, contains a few very likable characters and features a notable amount of suspense and splatter, but still the film is too ? decent! This is another horror movie intended for the modern day and mainstream target audiences, with an overly politically correct script and too little shock-value. Perhaps I'm just a nagging old-fashioned horror geek, but this is a wondrous example of a remake that completely lacks the gritty, nihilistic, disturbing and raw atmosphere that was so common in the early 1970's.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A completely worthwhile remake, it was CRAZY.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Tonight I saw the remake of 1973 film "The Crazies" and it was, actually, insane.Set in a small town in Iowa 'The Crazies' is not your typical zombie film, in fact zombies doesn't really describe the infected delights of this town. Using the traditional, 'widespread, contagious virus' set-up the storyline has the basis to be incredibly clichéd, and whilst it does have one of those really, you survived that? moments, it also has a fascinating level of realism. The actors, primarily Timothy Olyphant, Radha Mitchell and Joe Anderson perform with a surprising amount of conviction making the film more horrifying than a majority of others in the genre. The screenplay (Romero, Scott Koser and Ray White) also meant that the characters were believable and allowed for a surprising amount of empathy, and for once I wasn't left begging for them to hurry up and die already.The cinematography was an odd mix of wonderful composition, ripped straight from art house sustained shots and conventional Hollywood action shots. Combining this with some pretty impressive special effects, (realistic fire is always a plus in films) used in some pretty innovative ways it makes the film absorbing to watch.The only bit I'm unsure of is the soundtrack; it's either really cutting-edge, or really contrived depending on how you like your horror. This isn't a big enough deal to have any real impact on the film. The film could also have a bit more gore, not that I wasn't clutching my stomach at some points (the make-up is pretty impressive).Overall, this incredible film pretty much has everything, including some irony and some unexpected twists making it 101 minutes well spent. Oh, and try to say for the full time, because when you think it's over, it's not.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Crazies, The (2010) ** (out of 4)By-the-numbers remake of George Romero's 1973 film has a small town being invaded by some military men when a plane crashes and unleashes some toxic waste that is turning people into "crazies". The Sheriff (Timothy Olyphant), his wife (Radha Mitchell) and the deputy (Joe Anderson) try to avoid capture and get out of town before being struck with the virus. I'd be lying if I said I was a fan of the original film and in many ways I wasn't overly thrilled with 28 DAYS LATER, which in itself was pretty much a remake. This film here has quite a few good moments but the lackluster direction really killed the film for me and by the hour mark I was started to squirm in my seat from boredom and the final forty-minutes weren't any better. I was really disappointed because the film started off pretty well with the first appearance of a "crazy" being handled quite well. The rest of the movie went downhill even though a couple of the cast members tried their best to save it. This follows the original film pretty closely, although there's no question that Romero did a much better job considering the budget he was working with and at least his screenplay managed to do a few good things. This film has one rather dumb character after another doing one stupid thing after another but the entire screenplay is basically just a "copy what worked in previous movies". There's not a single original idea on display here and Eisner's direction really doesn't bring anything to life. He keeps the film looking professional but any director of this type of film should at least be able to do that. Each scene has violence, drama or some sort of gore but there's no passion or life behind it. It's just there on the screen, bland and not too involving. I thought Olyphant made for an interesting leading man and I thought he tried very hard to keep everything moving. He was certainly much better than the material as was Anderson as his sidekick. I really didn't care for Mitchell at all; her performance and character were annoying to me. The film follows just about every twist and turn you'd expect and in the end there's really nothing much here. It's certainly far from a bad movie but at the same time it's not a very good one either.</fullReview>
